The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Patay" title="Battle of Patay">Battle of Patay</a>,<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Arc" title="Joan of Arc">Joan of Arc</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Siege of Orléans">Siege of Orléans</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2">
<table style="width:100%;margin:0;padding:0;border:0">
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Date</th>
<td>1337-1453 (116 years)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Location</th>
<td><span class="location">France, the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, England, Spain</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Result</th>
<td>
<p>French victory<br/>
The Valois retained the throne of France</p>
<dl>
<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwardian_War" title="Edwardian War">Edwardian War</a> (1337–1360)</dt>
<dd>English victory: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Br%C3%A9tigny" title="Treaty of Brétigny">Treaty of Brétigny</a></dd>
<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caroline_War" title="Caroline War">Caroline War</a> (1369–1389)</dt>
<dd>French victory: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Bruges_(1375)" title="Treaty of Bruges (1375)">Treaty of Bruges</a></dd>
<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lancastrian_War" title="Lancastrian War">Lancastrian War</a>, 1st phase (1415–1420)</dt>
<dd>English victory: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Troyes" title="Treaty of Troyes">Treaty of Troyes</a></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1415%E2%80%9353)" title="Hundred Years' War (1415–53)">Hundred Years' War</a>, 2nd phase (1420–1453)</dt>
<dd>French victory</dd>
</dl>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Territorial<br/>
changes</th>
<td>France acquired the English continental possessions, except for <a href="/wiki/Pale_of_Calais" title="Pale of Calais">Calais</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>

<p>The <b>Hundred Years' War</b> was a series of conflicts waged from 1337 to 1453 by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</a>, rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>, against the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Valois" title="House of Valois">House of Valois</a>, rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">Kingdom of France</a>, over the succession of the French throne. Each side drew many allies into the war. It was one of the most notable conflicts of the Middle Ages, in which five generations of kings from two rival dynasties fought for the throne of the largest kingdom in Western Europe. The war marked both the height of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a> and its subsequent decline, and the development of strong national identities in both countries.</p>
<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a>, the kings of England were <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassals</a> of the kings of France for their possessions in France. The French kings had endeavored, over the centuries, to reduce these possessions, to the effect that only <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a> was left to the English. The confiscation or threat of confiscating this duchy had been part of French policy to check the growth of English power, particularly whenever the English were at war with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a>, an ally of France.</p>
<p>Through his mother, <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a> was the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a> and nephew of <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV_of_France" title="Charles IV of France">Charles IV of France</a>, the last king of the senior line of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Capet" title="House of Capet">House of Capet</a>. In 1316, <a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">a principle</a> was established denying women succession to the French throne. When Charles IV died in 1328, Isabella, unable to claim the French throne for herself, claimed it for her son. The French rejected the claim, maintaining that Isabella could not transmit a right that she did not possess. For about nine years (1328–1337), the English had accepted the Valois succession to the French throne, but the interference of the French king, Philip VI, in Edward III's war against Scotland permitted Edward III to reassert his claim to the French throne. Several overwhelming English victories in the war—especially at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Crécy">Crecy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poitiers" title="Battle of Poitiers">Poitiers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Agincourt</a>—raised the prospects of an ultimate English triumph. However, the greater resources of the French monarchy precluded a complete conquest. Starting in 1429, decisive French victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Patay" title="Battle of Patay">Patay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Formigny" title="Battle of Formigny">Formigny</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Castillon" title="Battle of Castillon">Castillon</a> concluded the war in favour of France, with England permanently losing most of its major possessions on the continent.</p>
<p>Historians commonly divide the war into three phases separated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truce" title="Truce">truces</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1337%E2%80%9360)" title="Hundred Years' War (1337–60)">Edwardian Era War (1337–1360)</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1369%E2%80%9389)" title="Hundred Years' War (1369–89)">Caroline War (1369–1389)</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1415%E2%80%9353)" title="Hundred Years' War (1415–53)">Lancastrian War (1415–1453)</a>. Local conflicts in neighbouring areas, which were contemporarily related to the war, including the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Breton_Succession" title="War of the Breton Succession">War of the Breton Succession</a> (1341–1364), the <a href="/wiki/Castilian_Civil_War" title="Castilian Civil War">Castilian Civil War</a> (1366–1369), the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Two_Peters" title="War of the Two Peters">War of the Two Peters</a> (1356–1375) in Aragon, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1383%E2%80%9385_Crisis" title="1383–85 Crisis">1383–85 Crisis</a> in Portugal, were availed by the parties to advance their agendas. Later historians adopted the term "Hundred Years' War" as a historiography <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> to encompass all of these events, thus constructing the longest military conflict in European history.</p>
<p>The war owes its historical significance to multiple factors. By its end, feudal armies had been largely replaced by professional troops, and aristocratic dominance had yielded to a democratisation of the manpower and weapons of armies. Although primarily a dynastic conflict, the war gave impetus to ideas of French and English nationalism. The wider introduction of weapons and tactics supplanted the feudal armies where <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a> had dominated. The war precipitated the creation of the first <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing armies</a> in Western Europe since the time of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> and thus helping to change their role in warfare. With respect to the belligerents, in France, civil wars, deadly <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famines</a>, and bandit <a href="/wiki/Free_company" title="Free company">free-companies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Routiers" title="Routiers">mercenaries</a> reduced the population drastically. English political forces over time came to oppose the costly venture. The dissatisfaction of English nobles, resulting from the loss of their continental landholdings, became a factor leading to the civil wars known as the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> (1455–1487). Shorn of its continental possessions, England was left with the sense of being an isolated nation, which profoundly affected its outlook and development for more than 500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_D._Cleva_p._87_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_D._Cleva_p._87-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_conflict">Origin of the conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin of the conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The root causes of the conflict can be found in the demographic, economic and social crises of 14th century Europe. The outbreak of war was motivated by a gradual rise in tension between the Kings of France and England about Guyenne, Flanders and Scotland. The dynastic question, which arose due to an interruption of the direct male line of the Capetians, was the official pretext.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynastic_turmoil_in_France:_1314.E2.80.9328">Dynastic turmoil in France: 1314–28</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Dynastic turmoil in France: 1314–28">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_claims_to_the_French_throne" title="English claims to the French throne">English claims to the French throne</a></div>
<p>The question of female succession to the French throne was raised after the death of <a href="/wiki/Louis_X_of_France" title="Louis X of France">Louis X</a> in 1316. Louis X left only a daughter, and <a href="/wiki/John_I_of_France" title="John I of France">his posthumous son John I</a> lived only a few days. Philip, Count of Poitiers, brother of Louis X, asserted that women were ineligible to succeed to the French throne. Through his political sagacity he won over his adversaries and succeeded to the French throne as <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_France" title="Philip V of France">Philip V of France</a>. By the same law that he procured, his daughters were denied the succession, which passed to his younger brother, Charles IV, in 1322.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brissaud329_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-brissaud329-2">[2]</a></sup></p>

</div>
<p>Charles IV died in 1328, leaving a daughter and a pregnant wife. If the unborn child was male, he would become king; if not, Charles left the choice of his successor to the nobles.</p>
<p>By <a href="/wiki/Proximity_of_blood" title="Proximity of blood">proximity of blood</a>, the nearest male relative of Charles IV was his nephew <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a>. Edward was the son of <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella</a>, the sister of the dead Charles IV, but the question arose whether she should be able to transmit a right to inherit that she did not herself possess. The French nobility, moreover, balked at the prospect of being ruled by Isabelle and her lover Roger Mortimer, who were widely suspected of having murdered the previous English king, Edward II. The assemblies of the French barons and prelates and the University of Paris decided that males who derive their right to inheritance through their mother should be excluded. Thus the nearest heir through male ancestry was Charles IV's first cousin, Philip, Count of Valois, and it was decided that he should be crowned Philip VI. In 1340 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avignon_papacy" title="Avignon papacy">Avignon papacy</a> confirmed that under <a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">Salic law</a> males should not be able to inherit through their mothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brissaud329_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-brissaud329-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orton872_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-orton872-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, Edward III reluctantly recognized Philip VI and paid him <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a> for his French fiefs. He made concessions in Guyenne, but reserved the right to reclaim territories arbitrarily confiscated. After that, he expected to be left undisturbed while he made war on Scotland.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_dispute_over_Guyenne:_a_problem_of_sovereignty">The dispute over Guyenne: a problem of sovereignty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The dispute over Guyenne: a problem of sovereignty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman" title="Anglo-Norman">Anglo-Norman</a> dynasty that had ruled England since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest" title="Norman conquest">Norman conquest</a> of 1066 was brought to an end when <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Plantagenet,_Count_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou">Geoffrey of Anjou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Empress Matilda</a> and great-grandson of <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>, became the first of the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin kings of England</a> in 1154 as Henry II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlett22_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-bartlett22-4">[4]</a></sup> The Angevin kings directly ruled over <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">more French territory</a> than the kings of France. However, they still owed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homage_(medieval)" title="Homage (medieval)">homage</a> for these territories to the French king. From the 11th century onward, the Angevins had autonomy within their French domains, effectively neutralising the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlett17_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-bartlett17-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_England" title="John of England">John of England</a> inherited the Angevin domains from <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II of France</a> acted decisively to exploit the weaknesses of John, both legally and militarily, and by 1204 had succeeded in taking control of most of the Angevin continental possessions. Following John's reign, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bouvines" title="Battle of Bouvines">Battle of Bouvines</a> (1214), the <a href="/wiki/Saintonge_War" title="Saintonge War">Saintonge War</a> (1242), and finally the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint-Sardos" title="War of Saint-Sardos">War of Saint-Sardos</a> (1324), the English king's holdings on the continent, as <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Aquitaine" title="Duke of Aquitaine">Duke of Aquitaine</a> (Guyenne), were limited to provinces in Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehistory_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ehistory-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The dispute over Guyenne is even more important than the dynastic question in explaining the outbreak of the war. Guyenne posed a significant problem to the kings of France and England: Edward III was a vassal of Philip VI of France and was required to recognize the sovereignty of the King of France over Guyenne. In practical terms, a judgment in Guyenne might be subject to an appeal to the French royal court. The King of France had the power to revoke all legal decisions made by the King of England in Aquitaine, which was unacceptable to the English. Therefore, sovereignty over Guyenne was a latent conflict between the two monarchies for several generations.</p>
<p>During the War of Saint-Sardos, <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Count_of_Valois" title="Charles, Count of Valois">Charles of Valois</a>, father of Philip VI, invaded Aquitaine on behalf of Charles IV and conquered the duchy after a local insurrection, which the French believed had been incited by <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II of England</a>. Charles IV grudgingly agreed to return this territory in 1325. To recover his duchy, Edward II had to compromise: he sent his son, the future Edward III, to pay homage.</p>
<p>The King of France agreed to restore Guyenne, minus Agen. But the French delayed the return of the lands, which helped Philip VI. On 6 June 1329, Edward III finally paid homage to the King of France. However, at the ceremony, Philip VI had it recorded that the homage was not due to the fiefs detached from the duchy of Guyenne by Charles IV (especially Agen). For Edward, the homage did not imply the renunciation of his claim to the extorted lands.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_the_war:_1337.E2.80.9360">Beginning of the war: 1337–60</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Beginning of the war: 1337–60">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-labelledby="Hundred_Years.27_WarEdwardian_phase_.0A_.281337.E2.80.9360.29" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War_(1337%E2%80%931360)" title="Hundred Years' War (1337–1360)">Hundred Years' War (1337–1360)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gascony_under_the_King_of_England">Gascony under the King of England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Gascony under the King of England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guyenne_1330.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="423" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Guyenne_1330.svg/220px-Guyenne_1330.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Guyenne_1330.svg/330px-Guyenne_1330.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Guyenne_1330.svg/440px-Guyenne_1330.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guyenne_1330.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
France in 1330.
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #4682B4; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> France before 1214</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #9370DB; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> French acquisitions until 1330</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFB6C1; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> England and Guyenne/Gascony as of 1330</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 11th century, <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a> in southwest France had been incorporated into Aquitaine (also known as <i>Guyenne</i> or <i>Guienne</i>) and formed with it the province of Guyenne and Gascony (French: <i>Guyenne-et-Gascogne</i>). The <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin kings of England</a> became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukes_of_Aquitaine" title="Dukes of Aquitaine">Dukes of Aquitaine</a> after <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> married the former Queen of France, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a>, in 1152, from which point the lands were held in vassalage to the French crown. By the 13th century the terms Aquitaine, Guyenne and Gascony were virtually synonymous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris8_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris8-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich298_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich298-8">[8]</a></sup> At the beginning of Edward III's reign on 1 February 1327, the only part of Aquitaine that remained in his hands was the Duchy of Gascony. The term Gascony came to be used for the territory held by the Angevin (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantagenet" title="Plantagenet">Plantagenet</a>) Kings of England in southwest France, although they still used the title Duke of Aquitaine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich298_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich298-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich292_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich292-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the first 10 years of Edward III's reign, Gascony had been a major point of friction. The English argued that, as Charles IV had not acted in a proper way towards his tenant, Edward should be able to hold the duchy free of any French <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a>. However, this line of argument could not be maintained by the English, so in 1329 the 17-year-old Edward III paid homage to Philip VI. Tradition demanded that vassals should approach their liege unarmed with heads uncovered; however, Edward demonstrated his reluctance by attending the ceremony by wearing his crown and sword.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson194_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson194-10">[10]</a></sup> Despite Edward complying, albeit reluctantly, the French continued to pressure the English administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich394_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich394-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gascony was not the only sore point. One of Edward's influential advisers was <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Artois" title="Robert III of Artois">Robert III of Artois</a>. Robert was an exile from the French court, having fallen out with Philip VI over an inheritance claim. He urged Edward to start a war to reclaim France and was able to provide extensive intelligence on the French court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich306_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich306-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Franco-Scot_alliance">Franco-Scot alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Franco-Scot alliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a></div>
<p>The Kings of England had attempted to subjugate the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> for some time, which made Scotland a natural ally of France. In 1295 a treaty was signed between France and Scotland during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip the Fair</a>. Charles IV formally <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Corbeil_(1326)" title="Treaty of Corbeil (1326)">renewed the treaty</a> in 1326, promising Scotland that if England invaded then France would support the Scots. Similarly, the French would have Scotland's support if their own kingdom was attacked. Edward could not succeed in his plans for Scotland if the Scots could count on French support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich304_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich304-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Philip VI had assembled a large naval fleet off Marseilles as part of an ambitious plan for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusade" title="Crusade">crusade</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>. However, the plan was abandoned and the fleet, including elements of the Scottish Navy, moved to the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> off Normandy in 1336, threatening England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich306_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich306-12">[12]</a></sup> To deal with this crisis, Edward proposed that the English raise two armies, one to deal with the Scots "at a suitable time", the other to proceed at once to Gascony. At the same time ambassadors were to be sent to France with a proposed treaty for the French king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sumption180_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-sumption180-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_homage">End of homage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: End of homage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the end of April 1337, Philip of France was invited to meet the delegation from England but refused. The <i><a href="/wiki/Arri%C3%A8re-ban" title="Arrière-ban">arrière-ban</a></i>, literally a call to arms, was proclaimed throughout France starting on 30 April 1337. Then, in May 1337, Philip met with his Great Council in Paris. It was agreed that the Duchy of Aquitaine, effectively Gascony, should be taken back into the king's hands on the grounds that Edward III was in breach of his obligations as vassal and had sheltered the king's 'mortal enemy' Robert d'Artois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sumption184_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-sumption184-15">[15]</a></sup> Edward responded to the confiscation of Aquitaine by challenging Philip's right to the French throne. When Charles IV died, Edward had made a claim for the succession of the French throne, through the right of his mother Isabella (Charles IV's sister), daughter of Philip IV. Any claim was considered invalidated by Edward's homage to Philip VI in 1329. Edward revived his claim and in 1340 formally assumed the title 'King of France and the French Royal Arms'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich149_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich149-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 26 January 1340, Edward III formally received homage from Guy, half-brother of the Count of <a href="/wiki/County_of_Flanders" title="County of Flanders">Flanders</a>. The civic authorities of Ghent, Ypres and Bruges proclaimed Edward King of France. Edward's purpose was to strengthen his alliances with the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>. His supporters would be able to claim that they were loyal to the "true" King of France and were not rebels against Philip. In February 1340, Edward returned to England to try to raise more funds and also deal with political difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich307_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich307-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Relations with Flanders were also tied to the English wool trade, since Flanders' principal cities relied heavily on textile production and England supplied much of the raw material they needed. Edward III had commanded that his <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">chancellor</a> sit on the <a href="/wiki/Woolsack" title="Woolsack">woolsack</a> in council as a symbol of the pre-eminence of the wool trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friar480_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-friar480-18">[18]</a></sup> At the time there were about 110,000 <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sussex#Wool" title="History of Sussex">sheep in Sussex</a> alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darby160_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-darby160-19">[19]</a></sup> The great medieval English monasteries produced large surpluses of wool that were sold to mainland Europe. Successive governments were able to make large amounts of money by taxing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friar480_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-friar480-18">[18]</a></sup> France's sea power led to economic disruptions for England, shrinking the wool trade to <a href="/wiki/County_of_Flanders" title="County of Flanders">Flanders</a> and the wine trade from Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sumption188_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-sumption188-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sumption233_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-sumption233-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak.2C_the_English_Channel_and_Brittany">Outbreak, the English Channel and Brittany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Outbreak, the English Channel and Brittany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BattleofSluys.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="555" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/BattleofSluys.jpeg/220px-BattleofSluys.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/BattleofSluys.jpeg/330px-BattleofSluys.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/BattleofSluys.jpeg/440px-BattleofSluys.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BattleofSluys.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Battle of Sluys from a <a href="/wiki/Froissart_of_Louis_of_Gruuthuse_(BnF_Fr_2643-6)" title="Froissart of Louis of Gruuthuse (BnF Fr 2643-6)">manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/Froissart%27s_Chronicles" title="Froissart's Chronicles">Froissart's Chronicles</a>, Bruge, c.1470</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward, with his fleet, sailed from England on 22 June 1340, and arrived the next day off the <a href="/wiki/Zwin" title="Zwin">Zwyn estuary</a>. The French fleet assumed a defensive formation off the port of <a href="/wiki/Sluis" title="Sluis">Sluis</a>. The English fleet apparently tricked the French into believing they were withdrawing. However, when the wind turned in the late afternoon, the English attacked with the wind and sun behind them. The French fleet was almost completely destroyed in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sluys" title="Battle of Sluys">Battle of Sluys</a>. England dominated the English Channel for the rest of the war, preventing French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasions" title="Invasions">invasions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich307_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich307-17">[17]</a></sup> At this point, Edward's funds ran out and the war probably would have ended were it not for the death of the <a href="/wiki/John_III,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="John III, Duke of Brittany">Duke of Brittany</a> precipitating a succession dispute between the duke's half brother <a href="/wiki/John_of_Montfort" title="John of Montfort">John of Montfort</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_I,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Charles I, Duke of Brittany">Charles of Blois</a>, nephew of Philip VI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogers88_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogers88-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1341, conflict over the succession to the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brittany" title="Duchy of Brittany">Duchy of Brittany</a> began the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Breton_Succession" title="War of the Breton Succession">War of the Breton Succession</a>, in which Edward backed John of Montfort and Philip backed Charles of Blois. Action for the next few years focused around a back and forth struggle in Brittany. The city of <a href="/wiki/Vannes" title="Vannes">Vannes</a> changed hands several times, while further campaigns in Gascony met with mixed success for both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogers88_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-rogers88-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Cr.C3.A9cy_and_the_taking_of_Calais">Battle of Crécy and the taking of Calais</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Battle of Crécy and the taking of Calais">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="969" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg/220px-Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg/330px-Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg/440px-Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%A9cy_-_Grandes_Chroniques_de_France.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Battle of Crécy, 1346</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="671" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg/220px-Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg/330px-Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg/440px-Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_counting_the_dead_on_the_battlefield_of_Cr%C3%A9cy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_III" title="Edward III">Edward III</a> counting the dead on the battlefield of Crécy</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In July 1346, Edward mounted a major invasion across the channel, landing in Normandy's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotentin" title="Cotentin">Cotentin</a>, at St. Vaast. The English army captured the completely unguarded <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a> in just one day, surprising the French. Philip gathered a large army to oppose Edward, who chose to march northward toward the Low Countries, pillaging as he went, rather than attempting to take and hold territory. He reached the river Seine to find most of the crossings destroyed. He moved further and further south, worryingly close to Paris, until he found the crossing at Poissy. This had only been partially destroyed, so the carpenters within his army were able to fix it. He then continued on his way to Flanders until he reached the river Somme. The army crossed at a tidal ford at Blanchetaque, leaving Philip's army stranded. Edward, assisted by this head start, continued on his way to Flanders once more, until, finding himself unable to outmanoeuvre Philip, Edward positioned his forces for battle and Philip's army attacked.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Crécy">Battle of Crécy</a> was a complete disaster for the French, largely credited to the <a href="/wiki/English_longbow" title="English longbow">English longbowmen</a> and the French king, who allowed his army to attack before it was ready.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich318_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich318-23">[23]</a></sup> Philip appealed to his Scottish allies to help with a diversionary attack on England. King <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II of Scotland</a> responded by invading northern England, but his army was defeated and he was captured at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Neville%27s_Cross" title="Battle of Neville's Cross">Battle of Neville's Cross</a>, on 17 October 1346. This greatly reduced the threat from Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogers88_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-rogers88-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogers55_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogers55-24">[24]</a></sup> In France, Edward proceeded north unopposed and besieged the city of <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a> on the English Channel, capturing it in 1347. This became an important strategic asset for the English, allowing them to safely keep troops in northern France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich318_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich318-23">[23]</a></sup> Calais would remain under English control, even after the end of the Hundred Years' War, until the successful <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Calais_(1558)" title="Siege of Calais (1558)">French siege in 1558</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grummitt1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-grummitt1-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Poitiers_and_the_Treaty_of_Br.C3.A9tigny">Battle of Poitiers and the Treaty of Brétigny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Battle of Poitiers and the Treaty of Brétigny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1348, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, which had just arrived in Paris, began to ravage Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In 1356, after the plague had passed and England was able to recover financially, Edward's son and namesake, the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>, later known as the <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Black Prince</a>, invaded France from Gascony, winning a great victory in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poitiers_(1356)" title="Battle of Poitiers (1356)">Battle of Poitiers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogers88_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-rogers88-22">[22]</a></sup> During the battle, the Gascon noble <a href="/wiki/Jean_III_de_Grailly,_captal_de_Buch" title="Jean III de Grailly, captal de Buch">Jean de Grailly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Captal_de_Buch" title="Captal de Buch">captal de Buch</a> led a mounted unit that was concealed in a forest. The French advance was contained, at which point de Grailly led a flanking movement with his horsemen cutting off the French retreat and succeeding in capturing King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_France" title="John II of France">John II of France</a> (known as John the Good) and many of his nobles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patourel20_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-patourel20-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson218_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson218-28">[28]</a></sup> With John held hostage, his son the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_of_France" title="Dauphin of France">Dauphin</a> (later to become <a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">Charles V of France</a>) assumed the powers of the king as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regency_(government)" title="Regency (government)">regent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guignebert304_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-guignebert304-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Battle of Poitiers, many French nobles and mercenaries rampaged, and chaos ruled. A contemporary report recounted:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... all went ill with the kingdom and the State was undone. Thieves and robbers rose up everywhere in the land. The Nobles despised and hated all others and took no thought for usefulness and profit of lord and men. They subjected and despoiled the peasants and the men of the villages. In no wise did they defend their country from its enemies; rather did they trample it underfoot, robbing and pillaging the peasants' goods ...<br/>
<small>From the <i>Chronicles of Jean de Venette</i></small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birsall66_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-birsall66-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reims_Campaign_and_Black_Monday">Reims Campaign and Black Monday</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reims Campaign and Black Monday">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="450" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg/150px-Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg/225px-Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/45/Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg/300px-Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_Monday_hailstorm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Black_Monday_(1360)" title="Black Monday (1360)">Black Monday</a> (1360), hailstorms and lighting ravage the English army at Chartres</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward invaded France, for the third and last time, hoping to capitalise on the discontent and seize the throne. The Dauphin's strategy was that of non-engagement with the English army in the field. However, Edward wanted the crown and chose the cathedral city of <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a> for his coronation (Reims was the traditional coronation city).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich326_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich326-31">[31]</a></sup> However, the citizens of Reims built and reinforced the city's defences before Edward and his army arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patourel189_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-patourel189-32">[32]</a></sup> Edward besieged the city for five weeks, but the defences held and there was no coronation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prestwich326_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-prestwich326-31">[31]</a></sup> Edward moved on to Paris, but retreated after a few skirmishes in the suburbs. Next was the town of <a href="/wiki/Chartres" title="Chartres">Chartres</a>. Disaster struck in a <a href="/wiki/Black_Monday_(1360)" title="Black Monday (1360)">freak hailstorm</a> on the encamped army, causing over 1,000 English deaths. This devastated Edward's army and forced him to negotiate when approached by the French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> A conference was held at Brétigny that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Br%C3%A9tigny" title="Treaty of Brétigny">Treaty of Brétigny</a> (8 May 1360).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patourel32_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-patourel32-34">[34]</a></sup> The treaty was ratified at Calais in October. In return for increased lands in Aquitaine, Edward renounced Normandy, Touraine, Anjou and Maine and consented to reducing King John's ransom by a million crowns. Edward also abandoned his claim to the crown of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patourel20_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-patourel20-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guignebert304_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-guignebert304-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsbretigny_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsbretigny-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_peace:_1360.E2.80.9369">First peace: 1360–69</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: First peace: 1360–69">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The French king, John II, had been held captive in England. The Treaty of Brétigny set his ransom at 3 million crowns and allowed for hostages to be held in lieu of John. The hostages included two of his sons, several princes and nobles, four inhabitants of Paris, and two citizens from each of the nineteen principal towns of France. While these hostages were held, John returned to France to try and raise funds to pay the ransom. In 1362 John's son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_I_of_Anjou" title="Louis I of Anjou">Louis of Anjou</a>, a hostage in English-held Calais, escaped captivity. So, with his stand-in hostage gone, John felt honour-bound to return to captivity in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guignebert304_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-guignebert304-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsbretigny_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsbretigny-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The French crown had been at odds with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a> (near southern Gascony) since 1354, and in 1363 the Navarrese used the captivity of John II in London and the political weakness of the Dauphin to try to seize power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner102_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner102-36">[36]</a></sup> Although there was no formal treaty, Edward III supported the Navarrese moves, particularly as there was a prospect that he might gain control over the northern and western provinces as a consequence. With this in mind Edward deliberately slowed the peace negotiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ormrod_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ormrod-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1364, John II died in London, while still in honourable captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman179_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman179-38">[38]</a></sup> Charles V succeeded him as king of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guignebert304_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-guignebert304-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica1_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica1-39">[39]</a></sup> On 6 May 1364, one month after the dauphin's accession and three days before his coronation as Charles V, the Navarrese suffered a crushing defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cocherel" title="Battle of Cocherel">Battle of Cocherel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner86_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner86-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="French_ascendancy_under_Charles_V:_1369.E2.80.9389">French ascendancy under Charles V: 1369–89</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: French ascendancy under Charles V: 1369–89">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-labelledby="Hundred_Years.27_WarCaroline_phase.C2.B7.0A_.281369.E2.80.9389.29" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">

<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Castilian_Civil_War" title="Castilian Civil War">Castilian Civil War</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquitaine_and_Castile">Aquitaine and Castile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aquitaine and Castile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="795" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg/220px-Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg/330px-Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg/440px-Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Du_Guesclin_Dinan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Bertrand du Guesclin in <a href="/wiki/Dinan" title="Dinan">Dinan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="849" data-file-width="1300" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg/220px-Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg/330px-Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg/440px-Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ofensivas_Tovar-Vienne_contra_Inglaterra_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Franco-Castilian Navy, led by Admirals <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Vienne" title="Jean de Vienne">de Vienne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fernando_S%C3%A1nchez_de_Tovar" title="Fernando Sánchez de Tovar">Tovar</a>, managed to raid the English coasts for the first time since the beginning of the Hundred Years' War.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1366 there was a civil war of succession in <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile#Ascension_of_the_Trast.C3.A1mara_dynasty" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a> (part of modern Spain). The forces of ruler <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Castile" title="Peter of Castile">Peter of Castile</a> were pitched against those of his half-brother <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_Castile" title="Henry II of Castile">Henry of Trastámara</a>. The English crown supported Peter and the French, Henry. French forces were led by <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Guesclin" title="Bertrand du Guesclin">Bertrand du Guesclin</a>, a Breton, who rose from relatively humble beginnings to prominence as one of France's war leaders. Charles V provided a force of 12,000, with du Guesclin at their head, to support Trastámara in his invasion of Castile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curry69_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-curry69-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peter appealed to England and Aquitaine's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Prince" title="Black Prince">Black Prince</a> for help, but none was forthcoming, forcing Peter into exile in Aquitaine. The Black Prince had previously agreed to support Peter's claims but concerns over the terms of the treaty of Brétigny led him to assist Peter as a representative of Aquitaine, rather than England. He then led an Anglo-Gascon army into Castille. Peter was restored to power after Trastámara's army was defeated at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Najera" title="Battle of Najera">Battle of Najera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner78_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner78-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the Castilians had agreed to fund the Black Prince, they failed to do so. The Prince was suffering from ill health and returned with his army to Aquitaine. To pay off debts incurred during the Castille campaign, the prince instituted a <a href="/wiki/Hearth_tax" title="Hearth tax">hearth tax</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arnaud_Amanieu,_Lord_of_Albret" title="Arnaud Amanieu, Lord of Albret">Arnaud-Amanieu VIII</a>, Lord of <a href="/wiki/Albret" title="Albret">Albret</a> had fought on the Black Prince's side during the war. Albret, who already had become discontented by the influx of English administrators into the enlarged Aquitaine, refused to allow the tax to be collected in his fief. He then joined a group of Gascon lords who appealed to Charles V for support in their refusal to pay the tax. Charles V summoned one Gascon lord and the Black Prince to hear the case in his <i>parlement</i> in Paris. The Black Prince answered that he would go to Paris with sixty thousand men behind him. War broke out again and Edward III resumed the title of King of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner122_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner122-43">[43]</a></sup> Charles V declared that all the English possessions in France were forfeited, and before the end of 1369 all of Aquitaine was in full revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner122_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-wagner122-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner3_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner3-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the Black Prince gone from Castile, Henry de Trastámara led a second invasion that ended with Peter's death at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montiel" title="Battle of Montiel">Battle of Montiel</a> in March 1369. The new Castilian regime provided naval support to French campaigns against Aquitaine and England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner78_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-wagner78-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_turmoil">English turmoil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: English turmoil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With his health continuing to deteriorate, the Black Prince returned to England in January 1371, where by now his father Edward III was elderly and also in poor health. The prince's illness was debilitating, and he died on 8 June 1376.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbbarber_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnbbarber-45">[45]</a></sup> Edward III died the following year on 21 June 1377;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbomrod_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnbomrod-46">[46]</a></sup> he was succeeded by the Black Prince's second son <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a>, who was still a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbtuck_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnbtuck-47">[47]</a></sup> The treaty at Brétigny left Edward III and England with enlarged holdings in France; however, a small professional French army under the leadership of du Guesclin pushed the English back and, by the time of Charles V's death in 1380, the English only held Calais.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vauchez283_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-vauchez283-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was usual to appoint a <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> in the case of a child monarch, but no regent was appointed for Richard II, who nominally exercised the power of kingship from the date of his accession in 1377.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbtuck_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-dnbtuck-47">[47]</a></sup> However, between 1377 and 1380, actual power was in the hands of a series of councils. The political community preferred this to a regency led by the king's uncle, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">John of Gaunt</a>, although Gaunt remained highly influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbtuck_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-dnbtuck-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard faced many challenges during his reign, including the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">Peasants' Revolt</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">Wat Tyler</a> in 1381 and an Anglo-Scottish war in 1384–85. His attempts to raise taxes to pay for his Scottish adventure and for the protection of Calais against the French made him increasingly unpopular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbtuck_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-dnbtuck-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_peace:_1389.E2.80.931415">Second peace: 1389–1415</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Second peace: 1389–1415">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-labelledby="Owain_Glynd.C5.B5r.27s_Revolt" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">
<table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit">
<tr>
<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r%27s_Revolt" title="Template:Campaignbox Owain Glyndŵr's Revolt"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r%27s_Revolt" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Owain Glyndŵr's Revolt"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r%27s_Revolt&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="Owain_Glynd.C5.B5r.27s_Revolt" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Glynd%C5%B5r_Rising" title="Glyndŵr Rising">Owain Glyndŵr's Revolt</a></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mynydd_Hyddgen" title="Battle of Mynydd Hyddgen">Mynydd Hyddgen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tuthill" title="Battle of Tuthill">Tuthill</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bryn_Glas" title="Battle of Bryn Glas">Bryn Glas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shrewsbury" title="Battle of Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalling_Down" title="Battle of Stalling Down">Stalling Down</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Grosmont" title="Battle of Grosmont">Grosmont</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pwll_Melyn" title="Battle of Pwll Melyn">Pwll Melyn</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_war_between_the_Armagnacs_and_the_Burgundians" title="Civil war between the Armagnacs and the Burgundians">Civil war between the Armagnacs and the Burgundians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="456" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg/220px-Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg/330px-Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg/440px-Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assassinat_louis_orleans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Louis_I,_Duke_of_Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Assassination of Louis I, Duke of Orléans">Assassination of Louis I, Duke of Orléans</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The war became increasingly unpopular with the English public largely due to the high taxes needed to sustain it. These taxes were seen as one of the reasons for the Peasants' revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker6_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-baker6-49">[49]</a></sup> Richard's increasing disinterest in the war together with his preferential treatment of a select few close friends and advisors angered an alliance of lords that included one of his uncles. This group, known as <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a>, managed to successfully press charges of treason against five of Richard's advisors and friends in the <a href="/wiki/Merciless_Parliament" title="Merciless Parliament">Merciless Parliament</a>. The Lords Appellant were able to gain control of the council in 1388 and tried, unsuccessfully, to reignite the war in France. Although the will was there, the funds to pay the troops was lacking, so in the autumn of 1388 the Council agreed to resume negotiations with the French crown, beginning on 18 June 1389 with the signing of a three-year truce at <a href="/wiki/Truce_of_Leulinghem" title="Truce of Leulinghem">Leulinghen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker6_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-baker6-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neillands182_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-neillands182-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1389, Richard's uncle and supporter, John of Gaunt, returned from Spain and Richard was able to rebuild his power gradually until 1397, when he reasserted his authority and destroyed the principal three among the Lords Appellant. In 1399, after John of Gaunt died, Richard II disinherited Gaunt's son, the exiled <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry of Bolingbroke</a>. Bolingbroke returned to England with his supporters and deposed Richard and had himself crowned Henry IV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbtuck_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-dnbtuck-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neillands182_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-neillands182-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curry77_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-curry77-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Scotland, the English regime change prompted border raids that were countered by an invasion in 1402 and the defeat of a Scottish army at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Humbleton_Hill" title="Battle of Humbleton Hill">Battle of Homildon Hill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mortimer253_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-mortimer253-52">[52]</a></sup> A dispute over the spoils between Henry and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_1st_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland">Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland</a> resulted in a long and bloody struggle between the two for control of northern England, resolved only with the almost complete destruction of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Percy" title="House of Percy">Percy family</a> by 1408.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mortimer263_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-mortimer263-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbbean_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnbbean-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Wales, <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glyndŵr">Owain Glyndŵr</a> was declared <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> on 16 September 1400. He was the leader of the most serious and widespread rebellion against English authority in Wales since the conquest of 1282–83. The rebellion was finally put down only in 1415 and resulted in Welsh semi-independence for a number of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnbsmith_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnbsmith-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the meantime <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI_of_France" title="Charles VI of France">Charles VI of France</a> was descending into madness and an open conflict for power began between his cousin <a href="/wiki/John_the_Fearless" title="John the Fearless">John the Fearless</a> and his brother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_of_Valois,_Duke_of_Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Louis of Valois, Duke of Orléans">Louis of Orléans</a>. After Louis's assassination, the <a href="/wiki/Armagnac_(party)" title="Armagnac (party)">Armagnac</a> family took political power in opposition to John. By 1410, both sides were bidding for the help of English forces in a civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curry77_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-curry77-51">[51]</a></sup> In 1418 Paris was taken by the Burgundians, who massacred the <a href="/wiki/Bernard_VII,_Count_of_Armagnac" title="Bernard VII, Count of Armagnac">Count of Armagnac</a> and about 2,500 of his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout this period, England confronted repeated raids by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirates" title="Pirates">pirates</a> that heavily damaged trade and the navy. There is some evidence that Henry IV used state-legalised piracy as a form of warfare in the English Channel. He used such <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateering</a> campaigns to pressure enemies without risking open war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hattendorf76_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-hattendorf76-57">[57]</a></sup> The French responded in kind and French pirates, under Scottish protection, raided many English coastal towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nolan424_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-nolan424-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The domestic and dynastic difficulties faced by England and France in this period quieted the war for a decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nolan424_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-nolan424-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry IV of England died in 1413 and was replaced by his eldest son Henry V. Charles VI of France's mental illness allowed his power to be exercised by royal princes whose rivalries caused deep divisions in France. Henry V was well aware of these divisions and hoped to exploit them. In 1414 while he held court at <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a>, he received ambassadors from Burgundy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnballmand_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-dnballmand-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry accredited envoys to the French king to make clear his territorial claims in France; he also demanded the hand of Charles VI's youngest daughter <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Valois" title="Catherine of Valois">Catherine of Valois</a>. The French rejected his demands, leading Henry to prepare for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnballmand_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-dnballmand-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resumption_of_the_war_under_Henry_V:_1415.E2.80.9329">Resumption of the war under Henry V: 1415–29</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hundred_Years%27_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Resumption of the war under Henry V: 1415–29">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-labelledby="Hundred_Years.27_War_Lancastrian_phase_.281415.E2.80.9353.29" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">
